Examples include clathrates, carcerands, and fullerenes. Common applications of cage molecules include:\n- Molecular encapsulation and guest-host chemistry (e.g., trapping small molecules or ions)\n- Gas storage and separation (e.g., methane clathrates)\n- Drug delivery systems (using the internal cavity to carry pharmaceuticals)\n- Catalysis (as nanoreactors or to stabilize reactive intermediates)\n- Materials science (e.g., fullerenes in electronic devices)\n\nMacrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules, such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins. Their applications include:\n- Ion transport and selective ion binding (e.g., crown ethers for alkali metal cations)\n- Molecular recognition and sensing (e.g., cyclodextrins as hosts for small organic molecules)\n- Catalysis (e.g., porphyrins as enzyme mimics)\n- Drug delivery (e.g., cyclodextrins improving solubility and stability of drugs)\n- Supramolecular assembly and materials science\n\nOverlapping uses include:\n- Host-guest chemistry: Both cage molecules and macrocycles can encapsulate or bind guest species, making them useful in molecular recognition, sensing, and separation technologies.\n- Drug delivery: Both can serve as carriers for pharmaceuticals, protecting drugs and controlling their release.\n- Catalysis: Both can act as catalysts or catalyst supports, often by providing a unique microenvironment for chemical reactions.\n\nIn summary, while cage molecules and macrocycles have distinct structural features, their ability to form defined cavities or binding sites leads to overlapping applications in host-guest chemistry, catalysis, and drug delivery.
